1594203334|4.0|After reading the alternately glowing and disparaging reviews of this book in various newspapers and in the blogosphere I really wasn't sure what to expect from this book: an interesting study of cultural difference, a how-to manual, a mad bashing of American motherhood? What it does offer is a well-written examination of both the intriguingly excellent qualities of the \\"French way\\" of parenting (which, quite frankly, didn't seem all that alien, merely more \\"old school\\"--it reflected my personal upbringing in many ways) along with the things that gave the author pause.  The author is certainly not a straightforward \\"convert\\" or evangelist for the \\"French way.\\"  She carefully parses the differences, both in terms of action and philosophy, between the French and American way of parenting, weighing the fears, hopes, cultural influences and expectations informing each approach.  Ultimately, in sketching out the differences, the author indicates that, by and large, both French and American parents desire the same things: happy, intellectually \\"alive\\" children (who sleep through the night!), and a degree of sanity for themselves (the parents).  How to arrive at these goals?  Here's what's got certain American mothers up in arms in the online \\"comments\\" sections.  But after reading the book, I'm somewhat puzzled over this response.  The author of Bringing Up Bebe isn't recommending many of the things these commentators are claiming (including that parents ought to ignore their kids, stifle them with arbitrary rules and force them to act unnaturally).  Nowhere in the book does the author indicate French children are ignored (quite the contrary--the author claims French parents are careful listeners and observers of their children) or unloved (is it really loving to indulge childrens' every whim?) or forced, for arbitrary reasons, to act unnaturally.  Rather the author reminds readers ofthings most people agree on: that, generally speaking, the happiest, healthiest individuals are those who experience and know how to deal with both structure and freedom, with both common courtesy (social rules) and personal interests and desires.  Negotiating between these positions is part of what being a member of any society is about (its important to note that the French view their children as a part of the social fabric, NOT as some American parents do: as some sort of not-yet-fully-human creature that, due to this status, has no role in or obligations to the world in which he or she is living).  At its best, what the book does do is offer drowning parents hope--a lifeline, if you will.  It speaks against the guilt and fear-mongering which is at the heart of the messages about parenting in America.  It assures harried mothers that taking a night off here or there to \\"be an adult\\" will not damage their children.  (It might even help them be better parents--staving off depression).  It offers truly helpful advice in terms of things like eating habits (children really can learn to like veggies!--french fries are a choice, not inevitable) which speaks to the very real problem of childhood obesity which plagues our nation.  In any case, however you think you feel about the French or the topics the author raises in this book, its worth reading with an open mind.  Who knows, maybe it will be helpful.  And even if it's not, it's an entertaining read.

B006IGZWB2|1.0|*** SPOILERS AHEAD ***<br /><br />I watched this movie and, for what it is worth, enjoyed it at the time. But upon further reflection, I think this movie is not very good.<br /><br />There are good moments in the movie, but then there are other moments that seem jarringly unrealistic. In another movie I wouldn't care. But in a film that at its core purports to accurately depict racism in the 50's / 60's, this starts to become a big problem for me later on.<br /><br />And yes, I know it is just a movie - not non-fiction. But with the amount of people touting this as an \\"important\\" or \\"life changing\\" movie, I think a bit of extra thinking is in order.<br /><br />One of the problems to me is that the main characters seem very shallow. Skeeter is the plucky young writer who is willing to face injustice, no matter the cost. Hilly is the EVIL racist who is set on destroying black people's lives. There's also an airhead blonde who serves little purpose in the movie, and a few bad mothers tossed in for fun. The black maids are all portrayed pretty similarly. They are all shown as good, honest people, many of whom raise and care for the babies of bad white mothers.<br /><br />Skeeter in particular seemed like a very unrealistic character. When the person who wrote the story makes the main character a writer, you know you are in for trouble. It comes off as self-indulgent as best. Mostly, it feels anachronistic. She's a liberal crusader for civil rights, and she arrived at this position how? From the maid being fired? I'm not buying it. Sure, there were some white people in Mississippi who cared about equal rights for blacks, but not many. And those who acted upon those beliefs were punished for it.<br /><br />And that brings me to another problem I have with Skeeter. She never suffers the true consequences of her actions. Never gets fired. Never receives death threats. Instead, she gets rewarded. She gets a nice cushy job in New York. The end of the movie sees her abandoningher black \\"friends\\" in Mississippi and heading off to a nice career for herself, never to have to worry about race-related matters again.<br /><br />What? You say her boyfriend dumped her for her beliefs? That was negative, right? Oh, puh-lease. That in no way compared to the kind of things that true civil rights advocates suffered. Losing a boyfriend would have been the least of her worries.<br /><br />That brings me to another thing I don't like in this movie - how it portrays men. The men are either non-existent, or fools. Buffoons or worse. Mostly, they just exist as props for the women in the story, who comprise the main characters. Nothing wrong with that, but I would have preferred for the movie to at least made some attempt to not show every man in it as a blockhead.<br /><br />The movie makes some attempt to avoid stereotypes and give the characters some depth, but still falls flat. I could not relate at all to most of the main characters. Maybe that's because I'm a guy, but I don't think so. It feels like weak writing.<br /><br />The writing went off the rails in a lot of scenes. One that stood out to me was when all the toilets were delivered to Hilly's front yard, and she has this big over-dramatic public meltdown. First, I find it hard to believe that people would dump that many toilets without at least knocking on the door. Second, I don't see a tough character like that bawling in public at all. But both events served the author of the story in her effort to make us feel that Evil Hilly got what she deserved.<br /><br />Speaking of Hilly... Bryce Dallas Howard stars, and man is she hot! God, what a babe. It's hard to believe she's related to Clint \\"Have some tranya\\" Howard. Dang, that guy looks like he was hit with the ugly stick. Okay, getting back on topic...<br /><br />There were a lot of things in this movie that didn't make sense to me. Like the whole deal on Constantine getting fired. I didn't understand why the black daughter wouldn't just goaround the back. Surely the white lady had entertained visitors before, hadn't she? And even if she hadn't, what's the big deal about being asked to go around back? And then when she does come in the front anyway, why is everyone so upset about it? It is viewed by the guests as an act of defiance, worthy of firing the black lady. To me, it just looks like a simple misunderstanding that the white lady would have easily explained and shrugged off. I'm supposed to believe that most white people back then were so racist that they never had any common sense at all? I don't know about that. The Constantine firing seems forced to me.<br /><br />And then later, we are told that Constantine has died, and Skeeter just takes it. Hello? She still has a daughter, people! A family! Maybe you could try to visit? Maybe even apologize? Nah, that's too much trouble.<br /><br />The movie has a lot of sentimental feel-goodisms that I didn't really care for. Like the cancerous mother who - inspired by her daughter - \\"decides to live\\" and suddenly becomes her own champion for social justice. Or Evil Hilly, who gets forced to eat it. Oh, and she also gets a cold sore at one point. Because that's what happens to EVIL people - cold sores!<br /><br />The worst part is when it takes sentimentalism and combines it with a lack of logic in order to create a result that makes you feel good - until you wonder what you are cheering about. A good example is when the maid steals a ring and tries to pawn it. We're supposed to feel sorry for her later when the evil redneck cops arrest her. Why? Because Hilly is EVIL, so to heck with her. And the woman really, REALLY needs the money. It's for the children. Also, she just \\"found\\" the ring, she it's not really stealing. Um, no.<br /><br />Yes, I know she stole out of desperation for her kids. And if Hilly had been caring enough or not-racist enough, she would have loaned her the $75 she needed. But wait a minute! We're supposed to hate Hilly because she won't loan the lady $75? That makes her racist? Hello, people! That's a lot of money for back then! Personally, I wouldn't even loan that much money to my brother today, let alone back then. Racism's got nothing to do with it.<br /><br />One of the odd things about this movie is the very premise of it. A white woman is trying to \\"help\\" black women by telling their side of the story. But the only thing she ends up \\"helping\\" is getting herself a nice a job and getting the main black maid fired (along with countless others, probably). So by helping these women, she is actually exploiting them. Am I missing something? Or am I just blind? Why are other people seeing a virtue here where I'm not?<br /><br />Let's face it. A book like The Help would never have been published, let alone become a hit. What white woman back then would have been interested in buying a book showing a black person's side of the story? The fact that the Jim Crow laws and racial segregation still existed shows that the vast majority of whites weren't too interested in what black people thought about anything.<br /><br />Another thing that irked me was how Abilene was fired at the end after Skeeter abandoned her, but we are supposed to be okay with that because Abilene says she's going to become a writer. (Again with the \\"Writer\\" Heroines... Sheesh!) I didn't expect a happy ending. But become a writer? Yeah, right.<br /><br />There's another thing worth mentioning. Many have taken to writing glowing reviews of the incredible acting of Viola Davis in this movie. That's much ado about nothing to me. She was okay. Not bad acting, but nothing outstanding either. I think people who do this are mostly white people who are trying to overcompensate and prove they don't have any internal racism by giving big props to a black actress. I mean, the woman is certainly talented and competent, but no more than anyone else in the movie.<br /><br />I will say this. My 1 star review is not fair. The movie deserves more than that. But my review won't get read if I give it a higher rating, so from my self-centered viewpoint, 1 star it is. There are plenty of 5-star ratings already, so it's not like my rating is going to make a difference.<br /><br />One thing I liked was seeing a few scenes from Clarksdale, Mississippi in this movie. Having been to Clarksdale many times, I thought this part was really cool.<br /><br />Back to the story. The main problem I have upon watching this is I don't know what to believe. The world has changed so much from the Jim Crow era that I'm unwilling to accept the history of it wrapped in a saccharine-sweet feel-good movie. I would like to see a movie depicting the real struggles that took place, without a bunch of sentimental fluff. I want the harsh truth. Not a sugar-coated feel-good summer chick flick. Guess I'll keep looking.<br /><br />I want to see the world the way it really was, if possible. Where blacks pulled themselves out of the hole through a lot of grit and determination, not the storybook movie lie where long-suffering blacks are finally \\"saved\\" by kindly, progressive white people. At least this movie made me think about that, and want to read more of the actual history. So that's something.

To stop any possible thoughts of rebellion, the Capital stages the Hunger Games every year. Each district is to send two tributes - one girl, and one boy, both between the ages of 12 to 18 - to fight to the death against all the other tributes. One person out of the 24 will survive. Whichever tribute survives will bring glory to their district, along with food and money that is desperately needed.<br /><br />The book follows Katniss Everdeen, from District 12, who volunteered to keep her younger sister Prim from being sent to the Hunger Games. Through her, we get a good look at what the Hunger Games actually mean for the Districts and the tributes. Alliances are formed in the Games but ultimately end because of backstabbing and survival. B006OFN0SE|3.0|Here is an interesting apples-and-oranges question: Which of the following is more pervasive, our desire to air dirty laundry in public or the powers of the federal government? In the early decades of the 20th century this was a silly question as \\"detectives\\" were unaware of such basic forensic science as fingerprinting and everyone agreed that a man's private affairs remained private.<br /><br />J. Edgar Hoover, the subject of Clint Eastwood's biopic, may have done more than anyone to create our modern America. Leonardo DiCaprio takes on this most difficult of roles in Eastwood's dark and detailed \\"J. Edgar,\\" a movie that somehow adds up to less than the sum of its parts. Hoover's legend just sits there, taunting the filmmaker. At 29, he took over the Bureau of Investigation and ruled like a czar for the next 48 years.<br /><br />In the movie's most successful scenes, Hoover builds the Bureau on science and zeal, fueled by his boundless ambition and the power of secrets. No agent could steal the limelight from Hoover, a true glory hound (all in the interests of promoting the Bureau, he claimed). Even though the Bureau nabbed notorious criminals and \\"solved\\" the Crime of the Century (the kidnapping of the Lindbergh baby), Hoover had no signature triumph or even a definitive foe. But he did fight and win innumerable power struggles with eight presidents. No one dared cross him. There is story enough for a good miniseries, much less a feature film.<br /><br />Then there is the juicy stuff about Hoover that you have probably heard, like his cross-dressing and sexual orientation. Eastwood gamely but respectfully explores Hoover's unorthodox private life. Young Hoover is an oddball, raised by a domineering mother (Judi Dench) and living with her well into his adult years. Hoover takes a young Helen Gandy (Naomi Watts) on an early date to the Library of Congress to show off the card file system he created ... and proposes. She rejects him firmly, and he responds by makingher his lifelong personal secretary. Hoover also forms a lasting relationship with fellow agent Clyde Tolson (Armie Hammer, in an under-appreciated performance) that is so close that the rumors of homosexuality seem like a foregone conclusion. The truth is much more complex. Mother, Gandy, and Tolson all loved Hoover in their way, but each relationship brought with it clearly defined boundaries.<br /><br />Eastwood has made a handsome movie (the period cinematography by Tom Stern is appropriately grim and dark) that ticks off all the major points of the Hoover story. But ultimately Hoover defies us all and remains a frustrating, unsatisfying subject to the end. Put \\"J. Edgar\\" next to other biopics that aimed high and missed -- \\"Amelia\\" and \\"Hoffa\\" are also on this list. \\"J. Edgar\\" may not miss by much, but if Hoover demanded anything, it was perfection.<br /><br />Extras on the \\"J. Edgar\\" DVD are limited to language options and a short film, \\"A Complicated Man,\\" in which the cast and Eastwood discuss the Hoover enigma. Review originally published on MyDVDInsider.com.

It's epic, punctuated by scene after scene which turns dark and twisted, and the protagonist is self-compelled, moving forward into hell without knowing why--because as evil exists, he feels drawn to it, to try to understand it, or if he cannot, to become a part of it. Guitar strings feel really detailed but vocals lose lipsmacking crispness, and there's not much detail in the bass.  Very efficient for non-amped used, but when amped, they sound fuller and cleaner and fast attacks (alternative rock) sound less muddy.<br /><br />I don't know if these have the acclaimed XXL drivers, but there is plenty of bass and an even amount of mids/highs.  Guitar strings sound great with detail.  Also, I don't know if they have that tuned port of the more expensive AKG's that reduce that bass pressure that builds in circumaural headphones. I think that these are decent phones for the super low price and even though people don't like high clamping force, it is high clamping force and more surface contact patch (possibly a square profile vs. a donut concave profile) that makes for better isolation and also, better controlled sound. B0078XM4SI|4.0|Roses is what I would call neoclassical music from The Cranberries.  After their debut CD they really started to emphasize more of a grunge sound - see \\"Zombie\\" or \\"Promises\\".  Roses takes me back to the simplicity of \\"Linger\\" or \\"Dreams\\" yet it is not simply a rehashing of their original material.  Quite the contrary Roses is a lot of different nuances woven together in a sonic tapestry.<br /><br />Long time fans will agree that \\"Tomorrow\\" would be right at home on Everybody Else Is Doing It So Why Can't We?.  It is the song with the least modernization if you will.  The next song \\"Fire & Soul\\" uses a drum/drum machine line that called to mind \\"Standing Eight Count\\" from Jacob Dylan's Women + Country.  Then \\"Raining In My Heart\\" has an accordion line that reminded me of \\"Long December\\" from The Counting Crow's Recovering the Satellites.  Other sonic variations include the use of strings in \\"Waiting In Walthamstow\\" and \\"Show Me\\".<br /><br />Other songs really grabbed me with their mood and emotion.  Both \\"Losing My Mind\\" and \\"Astral Projection\\" are dark and sad journeys into the frayed ends of sanity. While lyrical brilliance has never been the hallmark of The Cranberries, this is too close to what I call a seven/eleven song - seven words repeated eleven times.  It feels that repetitive.  These are not enough to ruin the overall experience of Roses, but it does keep it from being a top notch album.  There is definitely much more good than bad with this new collection of songs.  I think fans of The Cranberries will find a lot to like.

It's very easy to get confused as to which set has what features, etc. In this case, however, it worked out rather well for me, price-wise.<br /><br />This Blu Ray set is NOT the four-film \\"Complete Superman Collection\\" which was released on DVD and contained bare-bones editions of \\"Superman\\" I through IV, nor is it the \\"Christopher Reeve Superman Collection\\" which contained special editions of the original four films. No, this is the \\"Superman Motion Picture Anthology\\" (which was known as \\"The Ultimate Superman Collection\\" on DVD), the massive eight-disc deluxe set which contains \\"Superman\\" I (expanded and original theatrical versions) through IV, the Donner Cut of \\"Superman II\\", and \\"Superman Returns\\" complete with the original opening, a newer transfer and a slew of bonus features. This version of the product (the version I received, anyway) was from the UK, but that doesn't really matter since Warner Bros. does not region lock their Blu Ray releases (hence the \\"Region Free\\" in the item's description) and this set is identical to the US version. Amazon lists this set for sale under the correct title of the Anthology as well as under the incorrect moniker of \\"The Complete Superman Collection\\". Buying this set under this incorrect name saved me about thirty dollars over the properly titled one, so budget hunters take advantage! Now, let's go through these one at a time:<br /><br />SUPERMAN: THE MOVIE - The first big-budget superhero film and, in many ways, still the best of them. Richard Donner and Tom Mankiewicz set the template for every other superhero origin movie that has come since. And Christopher Reeve, of course, is still the ultimate film superhero. \\"Superman: The Movie\\" proved that comic books could be translated to a different medium and be taken seriously, if treated seriously.<br /><br />The first two discs in this set are devoted to the expanded and original theatrical editions of \\"Superman: The Movie\\". I like the expanded version of the film just fine, but I grew up on the original version, so I prefer that one. It just seems to move better, and doesn't introduce a lot of head-scratchers like Lois Lane being the little girl on the train in Smallville who witnesses a young Clark running along at super speed (how old are her parents, anyway?). The transfer on this version is a fair bit better than the old Blu Ray release, which I also owned and did not include the theatrical version. There seems to have been more work done on the expanded version's transfer than the theatrical version; there's not so much excessive grain and the colors really \\"pop\\" on my 51-inch. However, there's not a huge difference overall (the picture on a 30+ year-old film that was a bit soft in parts to begin with is only going to look so good), not enough to make me stop watching the version I love. Sound has been redone in DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1, and it really makes a difference to my ears over the old disc, which was given a plain Dolby Digital 5.1 surround track. Just listen to that subwoofer rumble in the opening scenes flying over Krypton. Whoo!! Bonus features are all ported over from the four-disc DVD edition, including the early George Reeves feature, \\"Superman Vs. The Mole Men\\"; none are in hi-def.<br /><br />SUPERMAN II (theatrical)/SUPERMAN II: THE DONNER CUT - Boy, the comments one could make about this debacle. In the interest of saving time, I'll just cut right to it: neither one of these movies is truly on the level of the first film. I love them (both versions), but it's a fact. With the first movie, you got to see Richard Donner's vision of the character completely realized. But then, of course, the Salkinds got cold feet and dumped Donner (after he had already completed most of the shooting on \\"Superman II\\", including Marlon Brando's and Gene Hackman's scenes) and brought in Richard Lester to reshape and complete the film. The theatrical version of \\"Superman II\\" still works well, expanding on the character and mythology of Superman while keeping the action quotient high. The cast is still great and Terence Stamp creates one of the all-time great villains in General Zod. Richard Lester just added a little too much goofy humor for my taste (dude blowing backwards on roller skates, etc.), something that would run rampant in \\"Superman III\\". Also, there's a certain affection for the characters that is missing from the final product; it all seems a bit workmanlike. And don't get me started on the producers eliminating Brando's footage just to save a few bucks after the first film had already made millions of dollars at the box office. Everything that was good about \\"Superman II\\" seems to have been sourced from what Donner and Mankiewicz had already planned beforehand.<br /><br />In 2006, after years and years of fan petitions and requests (a few of which I signed, I will admit), the missing footage from Donner's planned version of \\"Superman II\\" (including the Brando scenes, which help this version improve immeasurably on the theatrical cut) was found, restored, re-edited and released using John Williams' original music from the first movie. Watching the Donner Cut, you immediately notice how much more he loves these characters, how the movie just feels right in a way that the theatrical version did not. The replacement of the theatrical version's opening action setpiece at the Eiffel Tower is a prime example. Donner's opener, in which Lois jumps out of a window at the Daily Planet in hopes of forcing Clark to reveal himself as Superman and save her, immediately shows the big differences between the two versions: the plot does not drive the characters in the Donner Cut, the characters drive the plot. The Kryptonian villains are treated more seriously and possess real menace (see the scene where they storm the White House). The Donner Cut also benefits from the restoration of the luminous footage shot by the late, great Geoffrey Unsworth, whose work on the sequel was mostly lost to Lester's harshly lit reshoots. There were certain scenes between Lois and Clark where she confronts him over his dual identity that Richard Donner planned to shoot but did not have the chance, so screen test footage was substituted. And that's where it hit me. Therein lay the problem with the Donner Cut: it's not actually Richard Donner's \\"Superman II.\\" It's as close as one can actually get to what could have been the best superhero film of all time in \\"Superman II\\", but that film does not exist, will never exist. All we have, thanks to the producers' greed and shortsightedness, is a reasonable facsimile with substituted footage and a rehashed ending (Donner and company never got to think up a new ending for \\"Superman II\\" after using its big turning-back-time climax for the first movie). Lest anybody think I'm just piling the blame on the Salkinds (though they totally deserve most of it), Donner and the driving force behind the project, filmmaker Michael Thau, shoot themselves in the feet by refusing to touch ANY of the Lester footage except where absolutely necessary, which gives rise to some mediocre line readings and bad dubs which were actually improved upon in Lester's theatrical cut (the scene where Superman challenges Zod to a fight at the Daily Planet springs to mind). I can understand Donner's feelings about the project, but a little compromise in this case might have gone a long way. As it is, the Donner Cut is now my preferred version of Superman II, but not by as huge a margin as I might have thought.<br /><br />Discs 3 and 4 are devoted to the two \\"Superman II\\"s. The transfer on the Donner Cut seems to be the same as the original Blu Ray release, which was pretty good. The theatrical cut of \\"Superman II\\" (which I've never seen on Blu Ray) fares pretty well and, like the Donner Cut, seems sharper than the first film. Again, the main difference is in the DTS-HD MA sound, which is fuller and more robust than the previously available Dolby Digital 5.1 track. All bonus features were ported over from the two-disc DVD of the theatrical cut and the previously released Donner Cut, respectively. All of the Fleischer/Famous Studios cartoons can be found on these two discs, with the first nine 'toons on Disc 3 and the second batch on Disc 4.<br /><br />SUPERMAN III/SUPERMAN IV: THE QUEST FOR PEACE - And here come the sad parts. \\"Superman III\\" was the first film where Richard Lester had control of the production from beginning to end, and boy can you tell. Overly slapstick-y, negligent of the main characters (poor Margot Kidder is on and off screen in two minutes flat), and worst of all, BORING. How do you make a Superman film BORING?! The only person who comes out of the thing with any dignity at all is, again, Christopher Reeve, who really sells the duality of Superman's character here (including a scene where \\"dark\\" Superman literally fights Clark Kent in a junkyard). About Richard Pryor, whom I have admired since I was a little kid, I can only say this: as a film actor, he was a terrific stand-up comedian. The only other useful thing to come out of this movie was the computer fraud plot device later referenced in \\"Office Space\\".<br /><br />Full disclosure: when I was a kid, the only Superman movie we had on VHS was \\"Superman IV: The Quest for Peace\\" (God, I know, I know). I heard my dad talking about how terrible it was, but I was a kid and I figured what the heck, it's Superman! So, for a number of years, I had a hard time thinking that it's as bad as everyone says it is. Well, it is, it's terrible. But not as terrible as how Warner Brothers treated Christopher Reeve and the film. \\"Superman IV\\" was originally supposed to be a big-budget return to form for the franchise. Christopher Reeve came up with an engaging original story (the wisdom of Superman interfering in real-world nuclear politics notwithstanding) and the original cast, including Gene Hackman, would reunite to erase the lingering bad memory of \\"Superman III\\". Then Warner Brothers slashed the budget and script and shifted the property over to Golan-Globus/Cannon, purveyors of countless '80s cheesefests such as Chuck Norris' \\"Missing in Action\\" series and \\"American Ninja\\", and the rest is ignominious film history. Sadly, this would be Reeve's final take on the iconic character of Superman and, needless to say, it's not how his portrayal should be remembered.<br /><br />Discs 5 and 6 are devoted to these two masterpieces, and they receive better audio/visual treatment than they probably deserve. \\"Superman IV\\" in particular looks crisp on Blu, all the better to see the cheesy matte lines and wires. Both have DTS-HD MA tracks; \\"Superman III\\" receives a 5.1 track while \\"Superman IV\\" is relegated to plain 2.0 stereo. Bonus features of interest include a commentary by co-writer Mark Rosenthal and a bunch of deleted scenes on \\"Superman IV\\" (ported over from the Deluxe Edition DVD released a few years ago). They're not great, but at least they help to make sense of and flesh out the plot, such as it is.<br /><br />SUPERMAN RETURNS - Everybody seems to have a different take on this installment, Bryan Singer's homage to/continuation of the original Donner films. Some thought it was too reverent of the original, that Superman was too \\"emo\\" and that there was not enough action. I personally liked the film, although I think there is probably some validity to those criticisms. I still feel that Brandon Routh was a bit too young to properly portray a more seasoned Superman who has gone through the events of the first two Donner films, but he was pretty engaging in the role, handled the emotional aspects of the role with skill (which, to answer the \\"too emo\\" critics, OF COURSE Superman is going to be emotional with everything going on in this film, he's not some soulless robot) and generally acquitted himself very well in inevitable comparisons with Christopher Reeve. Kevin Spacey almost makes you forget Gene Hackman's version of Lex Luthor, edging closer to the malice and insanity of the original character in the comics. James Marsden (who jumped ship on the \\"X-Men\\" franchise with Singer for \\"Returns\\") does a good job as Lois' boyfriend, a guy who could believably rival Superman for Lois' affections. Probably the only weak link was Kate Bosworth as a bland, rail-thin Lois Lane who fails in every way to measure up to Margot Kidder's take on the character.<br /><br />Disc 7 is solely devoted to \\"Superman Returns\\", and it improves on the original Blu Ray in nearly every way. The transfer on the original disc (one of Warner Bros.' earliest forays into Blu Ray) was, to put it mildly, a little drab. Well, not so on this new transfer! We're talking reference quality here, it's that good. Every color pops and every detail shows; you can see the weave of Lex Luthor's crisp white suits, you can see the fine mesh of Superman's costume and the crinkles in the leather of his boots (and you can also see just how suspect the CGI Superman is during the shuttle rescue). But for once, the DTS-HD MA sound falls a bit short of what was already available on the previous \\"Superman Returns\\" Blu Ray: a lossless 5.1 PCM audio track. Audiophiles and collectors might want to hold on to their copies for that. Bonus features have been ported over from the original Special Edition DVD release, including a feature length documentary on all aspects of the making of the film. But even better (and probably one of the major reasons I bought this set), we get to see the original fully produced five-minute opener of \\"Superman Returns\\" in which Superman searches the remains of Krypton for survivors. I don't know why Singer felt the need to cut this opening, especially when it was already shot, scored, edited, the effects completed, and (God, I hate when films do this) some of it was included in the theatrical trailer. It was a terrific opening! I do remember reading an interview some years ago in which Singer spoke of his idea to include the opening in a future theatrical re-release; I guess he overestimated the public's interest in the film, as did Warner Bros., which gave \\"Superman Returns\\" a $175-million budget. In any case, I'm glad the people who liked the film finally get to see it.<br /><br />Disc 8 is strictly bonus material and contains \\"Look! Up in the Sky!\\", the previously released feature-length documentary produced by Bryan Singer on the history of the character, and a brand-new documentary--yet again feature length-- called \\"You Will Believe\\" which concerns the rise and fall (and future rise?) of the Superman franchise. Both documentaries are in hi-def. Also included are featurettes exploring the scientific basis behind Superman, the comic's mythology, a tribute to Christopher Reeve, and a terrible, terrible old TV pilot, \\"The Adventures of Superpup\\".<br /><br />Superman was the first superhero (and if you can't tell by now, my personal favorite--sorry, Batman) in comics, and the first superhero to make the leap to a big-budget, non-camp film. Richard Donner, Tom Mankiewicz, Christopher Reeve and others pushed the boundaries of the character and paved the way for every other comic book property in Hollywood to be taken seriously. It remains to be seen where the character goes next in movies (I have more than a few reservations about the new Zack Snyder film due out next year) but based on where he's been, Superman deserves much cinematic respect. And with this Blu Ray set, he gets it in a BIG way.

B00005BL2A|5.0|Of all the Depeche Mode albums from the post-Vince Clarke era, this one seems to get the least support from fans. If you follow Depeche Mode, you know they do tend to change things up now and then. They began as a happy-sounding synth band, gradually added guitars and became progressively darker (even going through an industrial period), shifted to a unique blend of grunge and gospel with Songs of Faith and Devotion, and went through another metamorphosis with Ultra. This album, released in 2001, is termed by some fans as \\"the love album\\" due to the nature of many of the songs. It's about as close to mainstream pop as DM ever got, although it's a different, more mature type of pop from its era (keep in mind we were still in the midst of the boy band/pop princess era in May 2001). I'll go through this album song by song to highlight each selection, especially to look at some of the underrated tracks.<br /><br />\\"Dream On\\": This was by far the most well-known single from the album (and was the only track to make it on the 2006 Greatest Hits compilation). The song is rather simple but very catchy. The album version is a little different from the single--it's longer overall thanks to an introduction and a slightly different song structure.<br /><br />\\"Shine\\": My personal favorite track on this album. This is an unusual song in that it's a ballad sung by Dave Gahan (most ballad-type songs are given to Martin Gore). This should have been a single--the lyrics are meaningful and memorable and the vocals are incredible (they spotlight Dave's high register quite nicely). The synths in the background create a great mood, too. If there's any one track you should purchase if you're getting a la carte tracks on Amazon or another music service, this would be my pick.<br /><br />\\"The Sweetest Condition\\": I've heard a few people compare this to \\"Sweetest Perfection\\" from Violator. While I can see a few comparisons beyond the similar titles, I can't say this song generates the same overall mood as the earlier work. Overall, a nice track, and one that spotlights the great harmonies Dave and Martin can create when singing together.<br /><br />\\"When the Body Speaks\\": Another slower song sung from the heart, again with Dave on lead vocals. While not as memorable as \\"Shine\\", it's still a great effort overall and essentially is the fourth and final segment of the first part of the album (the next track, as described below, is far different). My favorite part of this song is the background guitar, which adds a certain element to this song I really like. The ending of this song, with the soft guitar solo, is something that will stick in your head forever.<br /><br />\\"The Dead of Night\\": My first reaction to hearing this song: where did this come from? This song is completely different from anything on the album and seems like an Ultra outtake. To use an analogy: you've got all these clean-cut guys in suits (the first four tracks) and then the guy with the long hair, tattoos, and beater top shows up. The lyrical quality of this song is a little below the others and represents the first song that doesn't really have to do with love, but if you're looking for a heavy-hitting rocker, this song will be up your alley. Listen for Dave's haunting laugh near the end!<br /><br />\\"Lovetheme\\": The first of two instrumental tracks. DM got away from these for a while but started placing them on albums again with Ultra. This one's short and simple but provides for nice background music. Crank up the subwoofer for the opening beats!!!<br /><br />\\"Freelove\\": This is the third slow, gentle song featuring Dave on lead vocals. This is one of my favorites lyrically on the album. If you know of people who think of DM as a dark band with cynical lyrics, this song should change their perception (as will most on this album). It's a longer track, clocking in at over six minutes, mostly due to the lengthy instrumental parts. The catchy guitar from \\"When the Body Speaks\\" returnson this song.<br /><br />\\"Comatose\\": Martin sings this one. While it's not a bad song and Martin's vocals are excellent as usual, it's probably the weakest on the album from both a lyrical and musical standpoint. It's a bit too repetitive and underpowered in general to be a truly great song.<br /><br />\\"I Feel Loved\\": Ready for another curveball? This is an upbeat dance track with a heavy beat. Think dance club music here, not \\"I Feel You\\" or other earlier DM songs. The lyrics are simple, the music filled with loops, but this song turns out surprisingly good and is one of the highest points of the album. It's everything you'd least expect from Depeche Mode in one package. Dave's vocals are excellent here as well.<br /><br />\\"Breathe\\": DM returns to its roots on this one to some degree. This is another song with Martin on lead vocals and deals with something negative: rumors. Think of this as a modern, more mature, more organic, less repetitive version of \\"The Things You Said\\" from Music for the Masses with heartfelt lyrics. Martin delivers quite the vocal performance, showing the same passion present on \\"Home\\" from Ultra.<br /><br />\\"Easy Tiger\\": This is, hands-down, my favorite DM instrumental. The song sounds like something out of a movie, rather dark and foreboding in nature but not depressing. It's capable of painting vivid imagery in one's mind, no doubt due to its instrumentation and simple yet complex melody. The extended version from the special edition discs/online store compilations is definitely worth it.<br /><br />\\"I Am You\\": This is the most mainstream sounding track I've heard from DM in a while. While it's not as \\"happy\\" sounding as something from, say, NSync, it's something that would probably appeal to most NSync fans. There's some traditional DM lyrical devices in here, but this is, at the heart of it all, a great poppy love song with Dave's vocals as the icing on the cake. This happens to be another one of my personal favorites from the album.<br/><br />\\"Goodnight Lovers\\": The album closes with another DM oddity: a song in a major key (the overwhelming majority of DM songs are in minor keys). To sum up my feelings about the song: this is exactly what you'd sing to the girl who had a huge crush on you in middle school who you became good friends with in high school (yet you secretly liked her), then lost touch with during your college years. Suddenly, you're out of school and you rediscover each other/reconnect somehow, meet up somewhere, and realize you've been suffering all these years because nobody has come close to her. This song could practically be classified as easy listening; it's a very quiet, calming track. Yes, this is another ballad with Dave on lead vocals!<br /><br />Nearly one hour later, this great album comes to a close. B005CYLSW8|3.0|Unlike most reviewers, I can't summon a great deal of enthusiasm for this new \\"updated\\" set of Beethoven symphonies. Chailly has nothing significant to say beyond what Rattle, Abbado, and Haitink attempted in their very similar cycles - chailly's Beethoven is striking enough as fast, extroverted music-making without any intention, apparently, of searching for depth. I liked his way with the lighter symphonies (no. 1, 2, 4, and 8) on first hearing feeling that Chailly had found more energy and conviction in Leipzig than he had, especially towards the end, in Amsterdam. But I turned vaguely depressed and unsatisfied as I kept listening. The Eroica and the Fifth fell into the now familiar pattern of zippy tempos, punchy attacks, minimal vibrato, etc. The breaking point came with the \\"Pastorale,\\" where Beethoven's nature worship leading to the redemptive, hymn-like finale is rattled off without the slightest charm or tenderness, much less reverence.<br /><br />There is no special reason to extol Chailly over his rivals; even the punchiness that sounds arbitrary and aggressive is an echo of Harnoncourt's approach. For a great many conductors Beethoven needs to be \\"refreshed\\" because the music says nothing to them. Cleaning an old painting can  beneficial, but that's not what great music needs, since performance is a re-creative art that starts from zero with the first bar every time a piece is played. glib, efficient run-throughs aren't cleaning away yellow varnish; they are scraping off traditions that mean something.<br /><br />I can accept that styles move on, but it bothers me when Beethoven is no longer a vehicle for the ideals that connect the Renaissance with the Romantic era. Beethoven intended to be inspiring, heroic, defiant, humanistic, and revolutionary. Happily, we've gotten past the either/or choice between HIP dogma and Romantic -tinged expressivity. What counts in a great Beethoven conductor is insight, depth of feeling, innate talent, and the potential that lies hidden beneath black notation on a page. The fact that modernism rejected idealism naturally implies that old music must be poured into new bottles. I have no wish to cling to a raft out at sea as the ship sails on, but I hear little of Beethoven's real depth in these performances.hat lies hidden beneath black notation on a page. The fact that modernism rejected idealism naturally implies that old music must be poured into new bottles. I have no wish to cling to a raft out at sea as the ship sails on, but I hear little of Beethoven's real depth in these performances.

B004XNPQUO|2.0|This game has an interesting concept. You slingshot your projectile shot in an orbital pattern (that's affected by gravity) to blow up enemies, and trying to figure out the physics of how gravity affects your moves is an enjoyable cerebral exercise.<br /><br />As other reviewers have pointed out, there are some questions about how secure this app is. What is \\"sticky intent\\"? These are broadcasts whose data is held by the system after being finished, so that clients can quickly retrieve the data without having to wait for the next broadcast. Also, this game wants Adobe Air to open network sockets, which has been described as opening a backdoor into your device.<br /><br />Summary: The premise of this game is good, and somewhat novel in comparison to others in the App Store. Those who are concerned about security leaks from their device will probably not want to download this game, even though it's free today.

1594203334|3.0|I was entertained and amused by Pam Druckerman's book, Bringing Up Bebe: One American Mother Discovers the Wisdom of French Parenting. Druckerman presents her personal story of raising children in France. Working like an anthropologist as a participant-observer of the culture, she is able to call attention to the differences she saw in how French parents behaved with their children as compared to many American parents. Among the many differences she presents are the approaches to patience and waiting rather than leaping in, and the ways in which autonomy is encouraged. No one parent's experience or observation is ever definitive for another parent's behavior, so those readers expecting a how-to manual will be disappointed. 0596007124|5.0|I purchased this book about a year ago and devoted a number of hours each week going through it.  I am a software developer by trade, but never took a computer science course in my life, mostly because I was self-taught as a kid and all of this pre-dated the Web by about 20 years.<br /><br />In any event, one area where I knew I was lacking was a formal understanding of design patterns.  I was in the middle of a fairly large enterprise application, and while I was fully versed in Object Oriented Programming (and had even taught it in the past), I realized that everyone we were hiring out of college \\"spoke\\" a different language than I did, talking about singletons, factories, MVC, etc.<br /><br />This book covers those and many, many more, in a completely involving and revolutionary way.  It's basically designed with the Java language in mind, but I'm a .Net developer, never having learned Java.  However, the book is so thorough and intuitive, that I decided to do all of the examples in C# (close enough to Java that in many cases, there's almost a 1-to-1 relationship between my home-grown example and the one in the book.<br /><br />So for me, the book's benefit was two-fold: exposure to both design patterns and the Java language.  In many cases, I was able to \\".Net-ify\\" the examples to take advantage of specific features of .Net.  A good example was in the Observer pattern where you have an object that changes over time, and other objects (\\"observers\\") need to be aware of this change.  The book presents a language-agnostic approach, where the observable object registers all of its observers, and cycles through them notifying each one that it has changed.  With .Net, it's much easier (and more appropriate) to implement this using events, where the observable object doesn't care who's watching, it just announces \\"I've changed!\\" and each observer can then decide how to handle that event.<br /><br />But I digress... ultimately, I just threw myself into this book, doing every example as written, and then modifying those examples to function with the .Net mentality.  I even did all the pencil exercises, puzzles, etc.  It's all part of the learning process, right?<br /><br />So, in a nutshell, if you need to learn design patterns, get this book.  It's a lot of fun, and chock full of great information and examples that you'll be able to apply to your own projects right away.

0316069906|4.0|Eating Animals was first written in 2009 by Jonathan Safran Foer. It was his third novel and extremely successful. Foer's motivation to uncover the truth about meat is commendable and puts his work above the peachiness of novels that are more \\"anti-meat\\" and pushing the vegetarian realm. In Storytelling, when Foer first mentions that his book is not a classic vegetarian novel. The reader is left with the question \\"well, then what is it?\\" and this drives the reader forward through the novel's first chapter to uncover this mystery. Foer emphasizes a deep-rooted point that has been mentioned in other material throughout the semester. This point focuses on advocating a philosophy or belief through the food that we eat. Forks over Knives emphasizes the individual health benefits and political realm of each bite of food, while Fast Food Nation says the people have the opportunity to vote with their forks. However, Foer is unique in the sense that he pushes morality over other material that has been covered thus far. He states \\"not responding is a response--we are equally responsible for what we don't do. In the case of animal slaughter, to throw your hands in the air is to wrap your fingers around a knife handle.\\" However, throughout the novel his tone is personal, relatable and less than preachy. He covers some of the biggest problems right off the bat - waste management, superbugs, and animal suffering.<br /> One of the ways he accomplishes this tone is through storytelling rather than blatant regurgitation of facts. In one section, he blatantly exposes the animal associated waste problem. He talks about the holding ponds or \\"lagoons\\" that hold excrement and compares it to humans, saying the CAFO situation was as \\"if every man, woman, and child in every city and town in all of California and Texas crapped and pissed in a huge open-air pit...all year round.\\" America is drowning in feces. It is paying a high toll for the animals that are eaten. The lack of a basic large scale animal sanitation system is compromising our own systems. The blow is only made worse by Foer's news of the EPA reporting over thirty-five thousand miles of waterways contaminated by this inadequacy, and essentially driven by our insatiable indifference for meat. Through this excerpts language and tone alone, Foer highlights his role as a messenger and concerned friend. He tells the story as a neighbor would tell a neighbor. A politician would not say pissed or crapped, but the reader welcomes the honesty behind the message, an honesty that has not been expressed by the news, government, or food organizations. No wonder Foer decided to write a book about the findings of as a concerned father, the results are horrifying.<br /> While he covers excrement in great detail, Foer brings it a step further with his story focusing on the molecular biology and pharmaceuticals of agriculture. While this was touched on in some detail in other course material, Foer highlights the scope of animal's antibiotic demand. Livestock demand nine times more antibiotics than humans, twenty-eight million pounds versus three million pounds. No wonder America is experiencing high rates of superbugs. It is almost as if we are purposefully breeding them though our animal system. He talks about how the case of fluoroquinolone use in chickens opened a can of worms that has led to fluoroquinolone resistance. What is the most frustrating part of all this? The fact that we have the evidence and the tools for change, yet inaction overwhelms. America's animal consumption system is broken, but it does not seem broken enough to initiate national dramatic change. What has to happen before the government and corporations decided to do something about this crisis? Does an animal-based flu epidemic have to come around and wipe out thousands of innocent people? Does a majority of the waterways have to become contaminated? If there was a number or percentage that could predict when an issue was deemed \\"important enough\\" for action, it would be nice to know it.<br /> Foer's moral emphasis on suffering was what really made his book stand out from Fast Food Nation, Omnivore's Dilemma, Diet for a Hot Planet, and others. His focus is strong, the message clear. Either you don't eat meat, or you support a lot of animal pain and suffering. He offers little wiggle room. It seems that this fierce (however unobtrusively delivered) stance, is what causes him to make Michael Pollan an antagonist in the book, right alongside corporate giants Tyson and Smithfield. Michael Pollan's view is mild in the sense that he qualifies eating meat. He believes, like many others, that if a farmer has raised a chicken in an ethical manner, than the act of eating the animal is the natural order of the food chain. This is a view I have held for most of my life. Why? It is easy and it assuages our conscience enough to make eating meat acceptable. Foer brings to light an interesting point in terms of sustainable meat farms, like Frank Reese's turkey farm. He promotes the work of this miniscule one percent of the meat industry, however with everything he says Foer goes against all meat consumption. Therefore, it is interesting to note the difficulty that these \\"ethical\\" meat farms face. On one hand there are huge corporate farms appealing to the indifference of the masses, on the other there is an extremely conscientious and ethical way of living that argues a vegetarian diet. It seems that the vegan and vegetarian movements are the extreme side of a debate that has little room for the middle man. He is somewhat contradictory when he states in reference to Frank's heritage turkey farm \\"my wager [is] on a vegetarian diet...and [I] support their kind of farming.\\" It is not the best solution since small farms like Frank's could in no way replace the commercial system now instilled. Plus, how are the meat factories going to become more sustainable if most of the people who would otherwise stand up for animals are not eating meat at all? This applicable to the real world situation is where I think Michael Pollan's view has some traction.<br /> The moral component of Foer's book showcases two situations. The section where Foer proposes eating dogs as a sustainable meat source provides compelling moral ground against all meat consumption. He writes \\"no reader of this book would tolerate someone swinging a pickax at a dog's face.\\" This imagery makes the connection between the animals a family knows and loves and the animals that are hidden in the food system. It is not hard to realize that the people and animals that humans form a connection with on daily basis are those that are treated with morality and care. Someone could form the parallel between our families and people dying from AIDS in third world countries. If those afflicted people were in the backyard or living nearby, they suddenly become real and demand a better treatment. This same situation applies to animals. Thereby, it is extremely affective for Foer to take what is familiar to the typical American and then to inject the meat industry's morality (or lack thereof). He highlights that it is indifference that drives the meat industry. American society is okay to have its dirty work done by others in order to keep the personal conscience clean. Why do we treat our pets with such care and love but not our meat sources? Foer does a good job of emphasizing the point that is not in the way we raise our meat but in the end result. Our choices need to be about what actually happens to the animal. His connection between household pets and cows makes me realize the severity of our moral decisions regarding meat consumption. The reality that cows are bled, dismembered, and skinned while conscious is inexcusable.<br /> Foer goes into detail about the conditions and execution of pigs, fish, and cows. There is a complete lack of respect and morality that follows them into death. The section where Foer sneaks into aCalifornia turkey farm with the animal rights activist reveals these conditions. This tale-telling section is one of the most important parts of the book in terms of Foer's relationship with his readers. In this section, Foer gives readers something that most news sources lack. He reveals truth. He puts the reader and himself on the same side of the divide and draws the line. I like that Foer also is able to connect the choice of the non-meat eater to the common suggestion that vegetarianism reflects an ultimate sentimentality or child-like innocence. When he brings up the two friends ordering lunch, this does a good job demonstrating that the non-meat eater is actually portraying a responsibility to his planet and the environment that the meat eater disregards on impulse. This made me see vegetarians in a new light and with more respect.<br /> One unique aspect of Foer's novel is choice that he has made regarding his format in presenting the situation. In a way, he talks about very serious issues yet brings to the table a fun and playful attitude with them. This is appealing to the readers in the sense that no one wants to be depressed or made to feel guilty. They want an entertaining trustful friend to lay down the views. Foer accomplishes that with his story-telling and constantly shifting writing style. I think it is interesting that Foer never talks about dairy and eggs as part of his animal-advocate stance. It seems that would be an important topic in talking about the ethics of animal treatment, as it happens that many animals are made to produce milk their whole lives by being made consistently pregnant.<br /> Foer's heart is in the right place with this novel. He seeks to be a good father for his children and to reveal some aspects of the agricultural world. I like that he ends with a turkey-less Thanksgiving and brings it all back to the theme with his grandmother. The story that she was able to survive of the scrapes of others in Europe is an extremely compelling way to start and end the novel. Foer's stance that we are defined by what we are willing to do without is important but I do not know if it includes many things. What is the willingness to exercise on a daily basis? That is not the action of doing without something but rather adopting a new behavior. I would argue 'doing without' is in fact taking action--one of the most powerful ones that a person of affluence in a western society can accomplish. I feel a major emphasize of this course is our willingness to abstain from excess and live towards a simple more earth-friendly way of living through our daily actions. It is indeed an exciting journey that Foer has helped through his contribution, Eating Animals.animals are not eating meat at all? B000VFGQUC|4.0|The two disc DVD/CD set Live And Dangerous is a must have for fans of the pioneering hard rock band Thin Lizzy.  The DVD contains two live performances of the band, video clips, and interviews with the surviving members.  The second disc is a CD containing newly discovered live material from 1975.<br /><br />Although Thin Lizzy had various members, this collection focuses on the \\"classic\\" mid 70's lineup consisting of the late Phil Lynott (1949-1986) on bass, drummer Brian Downey, and twin lead guitarists Robbie \\"Robbo\\" Robertson, and Scott Gorham.  This version of Lizzy recorded the album [[ASIN:B000001F9E Jailbreak]] (1976), containing their breakthrough hit The Boys Are Back In Town.<br /><br />The main feature entitled Live And Dangerous at the Rainbow 78, was recorded in 1978 at the Rainbow Theater in London, and features the classic Lizzy lineup for what would turn out to be one of the last times, as Brian Robertson would soon fall out of favor, and be fired from the band.  Musically the performance is significant, as it captures this group at or near their peak, and clearly demonstrates Lizzy's ability to rock as hard as anyone.  Phil Lynott was a huge physically imposing guy, who dominated the center of the stage, flanked by Robertson on his right, the red clad Scott Gorham on his left, and with drummer Brian Downey very close behind.  These were some tough lads, not to be messed with.<br /><br />The video Live And Dangerous is great to have, but is kind of a disappointment in a couple of key areas.  The performance features 11 songs and runs about 51 minutes.  The CD version of [[ASIN:B000002KIT Live & Dangerous]] includes performances recorded in other cities, and has 17 songs, and runs about 75 minutes.  One might hope that more live video from the Rainbow is available, but apparently this is not the case.  Another issue with the concert video, is the jumbled order of the tracks.  The result is an abridged disjointed performance, that isn't a very accurate representation of Thin Lizzy live.<br /><br />In addition, the performance is not captured particularly well on video.  The stage isn't spacious, so the members are often close to each other.  The crowd is at the foot of the stage, and there are no camera operators directly in front at floor level.  Most of the views are shot from cameras at the sides of the stage, back by the drum kit, and elevated above the crowd at the front.  Camera coverage and editing reflects an older style, and isn't the best.  The camera operator on Robertson's side manages to nicely capture several of his solos, but the camera on Scott Gorham's side is out of position, so unfortunately when he solos, you mostly see Gorham's back and side.  Sharpness is acceptable but wavers at times.  Image quality is decent, but not great.  The audio mix often seems muddled and unclear, with inconsistent tonal quality.<br /><br />While the far from optimal, this is still a very enjoyable performance, for what it is.  You certainly get a feel for the power and energy of the band, and the magnetism and appeal of Phil Lynott, is evident even decades later.  Twin lead guitar players are nothing special today, but back in the mid-70's Robertson and Gorham, where among the trailblazers of this trend in hard rock.<br /><br />Disc One also contains an eight song, 45 minute performance recorded in 1983 called Sight And Sound In Concert.  This version of Thin Lizzy features ex-Tygers of Pan Tang guitarist John Sykes (Blue Murder, Whitesnake) teamed with Gorham, and Darren Wharton on keyboards.  This was apparently the band's first live performance after having recorded the album [[ASIN:B00005Y2EM Thunder & Lightning]] (1983).  Both on that album and here live, it is clear that Sykes pyrotechnic playing ability, brings a new fire and aggressive edge to the band.<br /><br />Although once again the camera work and editing is not the greatest, and the audio mix seems to lose one of the guitars at various times, froma performance standpoint, this is an absolutely killer show.  Commanding the stage, Phil Lynott is great as usual.  No doubt feeling some pressure, Scott Gorham steps up his level of playing.  Newcomer Sykes delivers big time, incorporating some flash into his playing, effortlessly duplicating his solos from the record, as Lizzy performs several songs from the new album.  This dynamic performance is a joy to watch, but alas much too brief.<br /><br />Rounding out the extras are several appearances on Top Of The Pops, the British equivalent of American Bandstand.  And a 45 minute interview which features Downey, Robertson, and Gorham recorded separately, and edited to form a fairly cohesive and comprehensive retrospective of the band, the music, and remembrances of Phil Lynott.<br /><br />Disc 2 is a CD containing Live At Derby, a recording of seven songs recorded in 1975.  The sound is a little raw and unpolished, but the guitar playing is very tight, and the music is worth listening to.<br /><br />Calling this DVD set Live And Dangerous, and using the same cover art as the album/CD, may not have been the wisest decision, as it leads one to believe that the music on the CD is also on the DVD, which isn't completely true.  Amazon currently regards the CD and DVD as the same product, which is completely wrong.<br /><br />Although it may be imperfect, the Live And Dangerous DVD is worth seeing.  Thin Lizzy was one of the pioneers of hard rock in the 1970's, and they deserve more respect and recognition, for their musical contributions.  This set, and [[ASIN:B002N7W3QC Thin Lizzy - Are You Ready?: Live At Rockpalast]] (2009), are highly recommended to serious Lizzy fans, and hard rock aficionados in general.a performance standpoint, this is an absolutely killer show.  Commanding the stage, Phil Lynott is great as usual.  No doubt feeling some pressure, Scott Gorham steps up his level of playing.  Newcomer Sykes delivers big time, incorporating some flash into his playing, effortlessly duplicating his solos from the record, as Lizzy performs several songs from the new album.  This dynamic performance is a joy to watch, but alas much too brief.<br /><br />Rounding out the extras are several appearances on Top Of The Pops, the British equivalent of American Bandstand.  And a 45 minute interview which features Downey, Robertson, and Gorham recorded separately, and edited to form a fairly cohesive and comprehensive retrospective of the band, the music, and remembrances of Phil Lynott.<br /><br />Disc 2 is a CD containing Live At Derby, a recording of seven songs recorded in 1975.
